    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5776642265607161715"><i>The Machine Stops</i> is a science fiction short story (12,300 words) by E. M. Forster. After initial publication in The Oxford and Cambridge Review (November 1909), the story was republished in Forster's <i>The Eternal Moment and Other Stories</i> in 1928. <br/><br/>After being voted one of the best novellas up to 1965, it was included that same year in the populist anthology <i>Modern Short Sto</i></span>
  <span id="freeText5776642265607161715" style="display:none"><i>The Machine Stops</i> is a science fiction short story (12,300 words) by E. M. Forster. After initial publication in The Oxford and Cambridge Review (November 1909), the story was republished in Forster's <i>The Eternal Moment and Other Stories</i> in 1928. <br /><br />After being voted one of the best novellas up to 1965, it was included that same year in the populist anthology <i>Modern Short Stories</i>. In 1973 it was also included in <i>The Science Fiction Hall of Fame, Volume Two</i>. <br /><br />The book is particularly notable for predicting new technologies such as instant messaging and the internet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5776642265607161715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/22982985-hristos-dagres"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1440435632i/22982985._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/22982985-hristos-dagres">Hristos Dagres</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This is a very interesting question. I was also puzzled by this. <br/><br/>I believe that this has to do with the de-materialization of human beings. Humans exi<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This is a very interesting question. I was also puzzled by this. <br /><br />I believe that this has to do with the de-materialization of human beings. Humans exist mainly as "avatars" communicating in an ethereal space - although they still keep their physical bodies, in isolated cells. Since humans lost their physical space and physical work is not required anymore, the only thing they produce is lectures and "ideas". Ideas are notions: the example of French Revolution is relevant. <br /><br />Each intellectual and each generation "purifies" the facts and events of the past, turning them in abstract notions.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

A polluted earth. The story talks about the dangers of the air. Forster wrote this in the midst of the very grimy Industrial Revolution.

              <span id="reviewTextContainer84615983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17632791815170804248">I don't suggest beginning to read this without first looking at the initial publication date. I was several pages in and scoffing at the oh-so-obvious-it-isn't-even-symbolism-symbolism when I decided to back-peddle and see just how old this story is. My cheeks flamed up a whee bit as I realized that <i>The Machine Stops</i> is just over a hundred years old. *foot placed firmly in mouth* A HUNDRED YEARS AGO Forster was discussing the cyber-age...a hundred years ago when the camera was some sort of madde</span>
  <span id="freeText17632791815170804248" style="display:none">I don't suggest beginning to read this without first looking at the initial publication date. I was several pages in and scoffing at the oh-so-obvious-it-isn't-even-symbolism-symbolism when I decided to back-peddle and see just how old this story is. My cheeks flamed up a whee bit as I realized that <i>The Machine Stops</i> is just over a hundred years old. *foot placed firmly in mouth* A HUNDRED YEARS AGO Forster was discussing the cyber-age...a hundred years ago when the camera was some sort of maddening breakthrough that Walter Benjamin hadn't even begun to be frightened of yet. Over 20 years before Huxley was warning of our <i>Brave New World</i> to come and <b>40 years</b> before Orwell foresaw the coming threats of the year <i>1984</i>. Decades before these masterworks, Forster had it more right than they would ever come close to being. My theory? E.M. Forster had a DeLorian.<br /><br />This short story tells of an age where everything you need is at the reach of a button. Food(think online grocery shopping and pizza delivery), friendship (think facebook, myspace, goodreads), music (think itunes, ipods, soulseek, limewire), literature (think kindle) and lectures (think online courses and shotgun online degrees from colleges of little or no repute) are all available from within the confines of your one-room home, which is identical to every other home in your entire bee-hive society (think suburbs, housing editions, the averaging out and synchronizing of everything so as to strip it of all personality). People numbly embrace this manufactured world, pushing button after button in monotonous satisfaction. Essentially, they live in isolation, dependent on the internet to provide everything for them. Exercise is shunned and the earth's surface is depicted as a frightening and useless place. A religious devotion surrounds the machine from all who are dependent on it. Sound familiar? <br /><br />As the title suggests, the shit hits the fan for this kind of existence. I don't want to spoil it for you, so I will just leave you with a question. What do you think would happen if our machine stopped? Would it look something like this? <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.southparkstudios.com/episodes/166179">http://www.southparkstudios.com/episo...</a><br /><br />This is a quick read which will solidify things which you (maybe, hopefully) know or have at the least given serious thought to at some point in your adult life. This story makes a similar suggestion to that of the amazing documentary <i>Surplus</i> and any number of sci-fi novels...that the machine will, in the end, be the destruction of itself if we allow ourselves to continue in excess, shunning things like real food, real love(making), real air, and real human interaction. We should maintain our reverence for the outdoors, for simplicity, for moderation. Step back and breath...do something on you own...take your bike that time...write a letter rather than an email, cook your own damn food and maybe even grow some of it yourself. Otherwise, it's all gonna go bust. Please note that I see the irony in ranting like this on the internet, and on a sort-of social networking site to boot. Hey, nobody's perfect, so piss off. All I'm saying is try a little. I'm trying to try! Try it.<br /><br />*steps off soapbox, exits review*<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17632791815170804248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2233124394" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2233124394" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2233124394?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cecily by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=scifi-future-speculative-fict">scifi-future-speculative-fict</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=short-stories-and-novellas">short-stories-and-novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2233124394" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7043908960314897043"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537360056i/26323701._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>Would you panic more at losing your wallet or your phone?</b><br/><br/>A few years ago, the question would be ridiculous, but now, it’s a tough call, and many of us fear losing our smartphone more, even with data synced in the cloud. Our world, and all our access to it, is there. I no longer know the phone numbers of my closest family. <br/><br/>We have outsourced our knowledge, and maybe ourselves, to our devices. Perhaps by recording my thoughts on this website, rather than trying to remember them in my head, I’m com</span>
  <span id="freeText7043908960314897043" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537360056i/26323701._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Would you panic more at losing your wallet or your phone?</b><br><br>A few years ago, the question would be ridiculous, but now, it’s a tough call, and many of us fear losing our smartphone more, even with data synced in the cloud. Our world, and all our access to it, is there. I no longer know the phone numbers of my closest family. <br><br>We have outsourced our knowledge, and maybe ourselves, to our devices. Perhaps by recording my thoughts on this website, rather than trying to remember them in my head, I’m compounding that. <br><br>As machines become more human, will we become cyborgs, and then machines - or worse, irrelevant?<br><br><b>Update</b>, January 2019: I was recently without a smartphone, and locked out of all data and apps for four weeks, and at a particularly difficult time. Sorting it out, and managing in the interim was far, far worse than the time my wallet was stolen. <br><br><b>Setup</b><br><br>This short story is told in three chapters. The first describes the situation, and has a hook as to what follows. The middle has someone questioning, and the third explores consequences. <br><br>The surface of the earth cannot support life, so everyone lives underground. Identical, solitary, sedentary lives, their every need provided by the Machine at the press of a button. People give and watch lectures, always on a quest for “ideas”, and ever grateful to the omnipotent and benevolent Machine. Each has their own Book of the Machine, with “instructions against every possible contingency”. That appeals to the technical writer in me. <br><br>Civilized people neither need nor want to be polluted by touch or smell. Contact with others is invariably by videocall, even between Vashti and her adult son, Kuno: <br>"<i>‘Parents, duties of,’ said the book of the Machine, ‘cease at the moment of birth. P.422327483.’</i>".<br><br>They have everything they need, but nothing that matters to being human. <br>“<i>Men seldom moved their bodies; all unrest was concentrated in the soul.</i>”<br>Do you have a soul when there’s so little opportunity for emotional engagement with anything, let alone anyone?<br><br><b>Only Connect</b><br><br>"<i>Only connect! That was the whole of her sermon. Only connect the prose and the passion, and both will be exalted, and human love will be seen at its height. Live in fragments no longer.</i>"<br>From <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24502863" rel="nofollow noopener">Howards End</a>.<br><br>Like GoodReaders, the people in this story are hyperconnected: simultaneously always and never alone. <br><br>“<i>She knew several thousand people, in certain directions human intercourse had advanced enormously.</i>”<br><br>Can one have meaningful friendships with so many? Can you even remember who’s who? Having so many connections surely dilutes the value of each one. Maybe hyperconnectivity makes us <b>more</b> isolated, despite the veneer of popularity?<br><br>Vashti has an isolation knob, mainly used when sleeping. <br>What if GoodReads capped friends at 500 instead of 5000?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537360056i/26323702.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Image: cover of Hawkwind’s concept album, The Machine Stops, from 2016<br>Title track (audio+video), A Solitary Man, <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=81bXCn49oRc" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.<br><br><b>God in the Machine, or God IS the Machine?</b><br><br>Humans may have created the Machine, but now it bestows life, sustains life, and chooses when to allow life to end. Reverence, rituals, and liturgy accrue to the Machine, and to the Book of the Machine. Eventually, the Machine seems to believe as well, and to institutionalise and coerce that faith, gaslighting as it goes. Humans subjugate themselves to the Machine their forefathers made to serve them. <br><br>“<i>We only exist as the blood corpuscles that course through its arteries.</i>”<br><br>I asked the same question about God and the Machine of Harlan Ellison’s 1967 story, <b>I Have No Mouth and I Must Scream</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2525764719" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>), last week. That also has people living underground, their lives controlled by an omnipotent computer, but in that, it’s the machine itself that usurps the mantle of God, and with very different consequences.<br><br>For a different and more comical slant on the apocalyptic danger of big brains, see Vonnegut's <b>Galapagos</b>, which I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57091859" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.<br><br><b>Edwardian Sci-Fi</b><br><br>EM Forster wrote this staggeringly prescient short story in 1909, set a few hundred years in his future, though we’re almost there now. He was 30, had published <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3087.A_Room_with_a_View" title="A Room with a View by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">A Room with a View</a> the previous year, and would publish <b>Howards End</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24502863" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>) the year after. Queen Victoria had died only eight years earlier, and The Great War wouldn’t start for another five years. A world or three away from now, even allowing for the technological and industrial progress of his age. I almost wonder if he had access to HG Wells’ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Machine</a>, as he describes things close to Skype, Spotify/streaming, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massive_open_online_course" rel="nofollow noopener">MOOCs</a>, online shopping, social media, virtual reality, and post-truth, fake news. <br><br><b>Quotes</b><br><br>•	“Just as the imponderable bloom of the grape was ignored by the manufacturers of artificial fruit. Something ‘good enough’ had long since been accepted by our race.” Note, the anonymous narrator says “<b>our race</b>”.<br><br>•	“I see something like you in this plate, but I do not see you. I hear something like you through this telephone, but I do not hear you.” Skype and virtual reality are not quite reality.<br><br>•	“The [previous] civilization that had mistaken the functions of the [airship] system, and had used it for bringing people to things, instead of for bringing things to people.”<br><br>•	“We have lost the sense of space... I determined to recover it, and I began by walking... and so did recapture the meaning of “Near” and “Far”... Man is the measure.”<br><br>•	“Man must be adapted to his surroundings, must he not? In the dawn of the world our weakly must be exposed on Mount Taygetus, in its twilight our strong will suffer euthanasia... that the Machine may progress eternally.”<br><br>•	“On atavism the Machine can have no mercy.”<br><br>•	"There will come a generation that had got beyond facts, beyond impressions, a generation absolutely colourless, a generation seraphically free from taint of personality.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537360056i/26323703.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Cartoon: Are we there yet?! (<a href="https://me.me/i/are-we-there-yet-almost-12191158" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br><b>Update</b>, May 2020. Will Gompertz, BBC arts editor, thinks we're there. He writes about the specific parallels with Coronavirus Covid-19 lockdown, <a href="https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/entertainment-arts-52821993" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7043908960314897043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1084391538" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1084391538" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1084391538?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1084391538" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3640006417588115925">My first thoughts on finishing E.M. Forster’s brilliant novella The Machine Stops, is that I cannot believe he wrote and published this in 1909.<br/><br/>More of a chronological peer of H.G. Wells’ <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine' title='The Time Machine by H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Time Machine</a> (1895) than of modern day science fiction, this nonetheless is downright prophetic in its anticipation of a global dependence on technological communication and the ironic social isolation and alienation that results.<br/><br/>Forster, better known for his realistic and modernistic contemporary fiction s</span>
  <span id="freeText3640006417588115925" style="display:none">My first thoughts on finishing E.M. Forster’s brilliant novella The Machine Stops, is that I cannot believe he wrote and published this in 1909.<br><br>More of a chronological peer of H.G. Wells’ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Machine</a> (1895) than of modern day science fiction, this nonetheless is downright prophetic in its anticipation of a global dependence on technological communication and the ironic social isolation and alienation that results.<br><br>Forster, better known for his realistic and modernistic contemporary fiction such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45195.A_Passage_to_India" title="A Passage to India by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">A Passage to India</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3087.A_Room_with_a_View" title="A Room with a View by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">A Room with a View</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3102.Howards_End" title="Howards End by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">Howards End</a> tells a haunting speculative fiction story about a world that had become completely dependent upon “The Machine”, a global network of living arrangements in which everyone lived in an identical box and communicated and existed through lifelines supplied by the machine.<br><br>Told as between correspondences between a mother and son, who live on either sides of the world and who, according to the mother, have no need to actually visit when they can speak everyday via the network created by the machine.<br><br>Forster has invented a cautionary response to Wells optimistic future expectations, one that warns against a breakdown of physical and actual connections in exchange for those presumed by technology.<br><br>Eerily relevant to our society over a hundred years later, this is a rare gem for a fan of science fiction and probably a surprisingly refreshing anomaly for a fan of Forster’s more recognized work.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555153900i/27357809._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="3640006417588115925" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_320129812" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/320129812" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/320129812?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/15831411">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=science">science</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=british">british</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer320129812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9749483193365620595">The Machine Stops, E.M. Forster<br/><br/>The Machine Stops, is a science fiction short story by E. M. Forster. After initial publication in The Oxford and Cambridge Review (November 1909), the story was republished in Forster's The Eternal Moment and Other Stories in 1928. <br/><br/>The story describes a world in which most of the human population has lost the ability to live on the surface of the Earth. Each individual now lives in isolation below ground in a standard room, with all bodily and spiritual needs met </span>
  <span id="freeText9749483193365620595" style="display:none">The Machine Stops, E.M. Forster<br /><br />The Machine Stops, is a science fiction short story by E. M. Forster. After initial publication in The Oxford and Cambridge Review (November 1909), the story was republished in Forster's The Eternal Moment and Other Stories in 1928. <br /><br />The story describes a world in which most of the human population has lost the ability to live on the surface of the Earth. Each individual now lives in isolation below ground in a standard room, with all bodily and spiritual needs met by the omnipotent, global Machine. Travel is permitted, but is unpopular and rarely necessary. Communication is made via a kind of instant messaging/video conferencing machine with which people conduct their only activity: the sharing of ideas and what passes for knowledge.<br /><br />The two main characters, Vashti and her son Kuno, live on opposite sides of the world. Vashti is content with her life, which, like most inhabitants of the world, she spends producing and endlessly discussing secondhand 'ideas'. Kuno, however, is a sensualist and a rebel. He persuades a reluctant Vashti to endure the journey (and the resultant unwelcome personal interaction) to his room. There, he tells her of his disenchantment with the sanitised, mechanical world. ...<br /><br />عنوانها: ماشین میایستد؛ روزی که ماشینها میمیرند؛ ماشین از کار میافتد؛ نویسنده: ادوارد مورگان فورستر؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دوازدهم ماه آوریل سال 1992میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: روزی که ماشینها میمیرند؛ نوشته: ادوارد مورگان فورستر؛ مترجم: محمود علیمحمدی؛ تهران، امیرکبیر، شکوفه، 1370، در 64ص، موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان انگلیسی اوایل سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: ماشین از کار میافتد؛ نوشته ای.ام. فورستر؛ مترجم: علی منصوری؛ تهران، روزگارنو، 1393، در 128ص، شابک 9786006867229؛<br /><br />ماشین از کار میافتد، شامل دو داستان کوتاه انگلیسی سده 20م، اثر «ای.ام. فورستر»، است؛ عنوان داستان‌ها: «ماشین از کار می‌افتد» و «اتوبوس آسمانی»؛ در داستان «ماشین از کار می‌افتد» در قالب روایت‌ ماجراهای مادری با فرزندش «کیونو» که به مثابة آیینه و تصویری از شربت است، گرفتار آمدن در زندگی ماشینی به تصویر کشیده می‌شود؛ داستان «اتوبوس آسمانی» ماجرای پسری اهل «سوربیتون» است که پدری خردمند دارد که سرپرست کلیسا و کاندیدای شورای روستا است؛ در روبروی خانه ی پسر، تابلویی با نوشته ی «به سوی آسمان» قرار داشت؛ پسر با کنجکاوی فراوان، درباره ی تابلو و اتوبوسی که در جلوی چشمان وی ظاهر می‌شد، سفر خود را آغاز می‌کند و این در حالی است که پدر و مادر، سفر او را سفری ناممکن می‌دانستند<br /><br />ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_2167581532" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2167581532" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2167581532?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2167581532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17041714522734926558"><b>Beware the New Scholasticism</b><br/><br/>The Machine Stops, written in 1909, is certainly a remarkably prescient tale of technological development. Like a proto-Cryptonomicon, it introduces ideas that we can now identify with the internet, the iPad, and even the 3-D production of goods, including food, from information. But its lasting value isn't about technology; it's about the mistakes we make when we start to think in a particular way. The biggest mistake is that of what we have come to call fake news.<br/><br/>Fa</span>
  <span id="freeText17041714522734926558" style="display:none"><b>Beware the New Scholasticism</b><br /><br />The Machine Stops, written in 1909, is certainly a remarkably prescient tale of technological development. Like a proto-Cryptonomicon, it introduces ideas that we can now identify with the internet, the iPad, and even the 3-D production of goods, including food, from information. But its lasting value isn't about technology; it's about the mistakes we make when we start to think in a particular way. The biggest mistake is that of what we have come to call fake news.<br /><br />Fake news is nothing new. But it is not merely unsubstantiated rumour. Fake news is that which confirms our existing views about the world. It consists of facts which cannot be gainsaid because no other facts are sufficient to displace the views we have already committed to. And it exists historically most markedly in societies in which established power is threatened.<br /><br />There is an historical epoch that was in fact dominated by fake news, the Middle Ages. This was the era of Scholasticism, a mode of thinking that prided itself in summarising the implications of what was already known about the world and making sure nothing else, particularly if it disturbed established views, could be known. <br /><br />In this, Scholasticism served the establishment of the Christian Church. Revelation, according to church doctrine, had been completed at the death and resurrection of Christ. This was the ultimate knowledge available to humanity. Nothing more was necessary. Further factual information or experience was at best superfluous and at worst distracted from the import of doctrine, which might be extended by inference but never altered.<br /><br />Forster's fictional world is one of technological Scholasticism. It is a world of "undenominational Mechanism." New experiences are considered not only unnecessary but positively harmful to this new religion. Vashti, the protagonist, " is seized with the terrors of direct experience." She and her fellow-travellers on long distance air ships refuse even to look out at the Himalayas since the sight "gives them no ideas." In good scholastic tradition, the only valid ideas are those that can be inferred from existing knowledge:<br /><blockquote>"First-hand ideas do not really exist. They are but the physical impressions produced by love and fear, and on this gross foundation who could erect a philosophy? Let your ideas be second-hand, and if possible tenth-hand, for then they will be far removed from that disturbing element -direct observation."</blockquote><br /><br />Our modern information technology is the vehicle for just this tenth-hand news. And the effects are similar to those that affected Forster's dystopia as well as the latter Middle Ages. We have experienced the same growing tensions between the governed and their governors; the same rise in extremist views and violent clashes among those who have adopted them, and the same yearning in many parts for the good old days of permanent, unchanging truth about the world. <br /><br />I suggest as a rule of thumb: any news that claims historical continuity, from any quarter whatsoever, is probably fake. Conservative politicians call it ‘family values’; the Catholic Church calls it ‘tradition’; Protestants call it the ‘fundamentals’; scientists call it ‘established theory’; Silicon Valley and Madison Avenue call it ‘improvement’; anyone over 60 calls it ‘yesterday’. All fake and all directed toward the maintenance of power. As Forster says, fake news is undenomenational.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17041714522734926558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2870027106362245835"><b>
  <i>"The saddest aspect of life now is that science gathers knowledge faster than society gathers wisdom."</i>
</b><br/>—Isaac Asimov<br/><br/>This is a short sci-fi story in which English humanist author, E. M. Forster, astonishingly predicts the internet age way back in the early 1900s (when a selfie would have left you with a blast of magnesium dust all over your face).<br/>In this dystopian future, people no longer get together for a chinwag over a mug of coffee; instead, they are hermetically sealed into rooms that res</span>
  <span id="freeText2870027106362245835" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"The saddest aspect of life now is that science gathers knowledge faster than society gathers wisdom."</i>
</b><br />—Isaac Asimov<br /><br />This is a short sci-fi story in which English humanist author, E. M. Forster, astonishingly predicts the internet age way back in the early 1900s (when a selfie would have left you with a blast of magnesium dust all over your face).<br />In this dystopian future, people no longer get together for a chinwag over a mug of coffee; instead, they are hermetically sealed into rooms that resemble cells in a beehive and only communicate via monitors. Gosh, imagine such a thing ... spending all day talking into a little screen! The world is controlled by technology, so face-to-face social interaction and, gulp, <i>touching</i> each other has long since flown out of the window.<br />All human needs are mechanically catered for at the press of a button and main character, Vashti, has no need to even get up out of her armchair, for it can glide across her hexagonal floor as easily as could a Dalek. <br /><br />And in the midst of this nightmarish vision, Forster offers the reader a bouquet of sumptuous prose to ease the cheerlessness of our future world, and to remind us of what humankind will one day forfeit...<br /><br /><i>It was night. For a moment she saw the coast of Sumatra edged by the phosphorescence of waves and crowded by lighthouses, still sending forth their disregarded beams.</i><br /><br /> *sigh* beautiful, yet so desperately sad. <br />Yet, for all Forster's remarkable clairvoyance, he does fail to predict that Peking would not be called Peking in the future (or will it?). Nostradamus would've known that, just like he'd have foreseen that Bedford Falls would become Pottersville if George Bailey had never been born. : )<br /><br />Wow! For having such vision and possessing such astonishing prescience I couldn't possibly award this work of genius anything less than five stars.<br />If I owned one, I would take my hat off to E. M. Forster.<br /> Of course, in the future, there would be a machine on hand to do that for me!</span>

<div id="review_3205855855" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3205855855" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73431206-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525868884p2/73431206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3205855855?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/73431206-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/38200651-the-machine-stops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73431206-richard?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3205855855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14384706034150360330">The Machine Stops is a Sci fi novella by the famous author of A Room with a View, A Passage to India and Howards End.<br/>I became aware of this quirky, atypical work many years ago when studying Howards End at school. I made a note to read it then and have finally got round to it ........... 40 years later!<br/>The story concerns Vashti, a woman living on her own in a hexagonal room under the surface of the earth. Everybody leads an isolated existence, in identical rooms, their every need catered for by </span>
  <span id="freeText14384706034150360330" style="display:none">The Machine Stops is a Sci fi novella by the famous author of A Room with a View, A Passage to India and Howards End.<br />I became aware of this quirky, atypical work many years ago when studying Howards End at school. I made a note to read it then and have finally got round to it ........... 40 years later!<br />The story concerns Vashti, a woman living on her own in a hexagonal room under the surface of the earth. Everybody leads an isolated existence, in identical rooms, their every need catered for by ‘the machine’. The machine, a quietly humming presence in the background was originally created by humans but has now evolved and taken control of the world. <br />Vashti is contacted by her son Kuno who lives thousands of miles away in an identical room and needs to see his mother, face to face ......... a shocking, unheard of request and a very unsettling one. What could be the matter? What cannot be said through the machine? <br />Direct contact and direct experience is firmly discouraged.<br />The Machine Stops is an amazing work of imagination. Amazing, as the novel foresees the internet, Skype-like communication, social media, driverless cars, internet shopping, Alexa-like control of household functions - the list goes on ....... and all written/ imagined in 1909!!<br />The near total isolation of everybody in this cautionary tale, connected only by screens, forms an eerie parallel to the strange and frightening times we are now living in.<br />The language is a little archaic and strangely, the text, on kindle, is strewn with spelling and punctuation errors. The book was very inexpensive so I can’t complain but it would be interesting to know how the text is set and finds its way into kindle format in such raw way.<br />A much recommended curiosity by this classic author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14384706034150360330" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2125761643" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2125761643" />
    <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya"><img alt="Leonard Gaya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442984307p2/27098959.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2125761643?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leonard Gaya" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya">Leonard Gaya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/32939893-the-machine-stops">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2125761643" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16446620383079529798"><i>The Machine Stops</i> is a three chapters novelette written in 1909 by the author of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45195.A_Passage_to_India' title='A Passage to India by E.M. Forster' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Passage to India</a>; probably one of the earliest dystopian works of the 20th century, before <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5129.Brave_New_World' title='Brave New World by Aldous Huxley' rel='nofollow noopener'>Brave New World</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984' title='1984 by George Orwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>1984</a>.<br/><br/>It tells, in a few brush-strokes, the story of a son and his mother in a world, far in the future, where humans on the whole planet live in sterilised and isolated cells underground, that they rarely leave. They reluctantly meet each other in person and prefer communicating through a network that fore</span>
  <span id="freeText16446620383079529798" style="display:none"><i>The Machine Stops</i> is a three chapters novelette written in 1909 by the author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45195.A_Passage_to_India" title="A Passage to India by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">A Passage to India</a>; probably one of the earliest dystopian works of the 20th century, before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5129.Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World by Aldous Huxley" rel="nofollow noopener">Brave New World</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984" title="1984 by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">1984</a>.<br /><br />It tells, in a few brush-strokes, the story of a son and his mother in a world, far in the future, where humans on the whole planet live in sterilised and isolated cells underground, that they rarely leave. They reluctantly meet each other in person and prefer communicating through a network that foreshadows our modern internet and messaging technology. They exchange ideas, preferably second-hand “and if possible tenth-hand” since they don’t trust direct experience. The mysterious and almighty “Machine”, to which one can access through a book — a tablet? —, tends to all the needs of this human hive. As says Forster, “Humanity, in its desire for comfort, had over-reached itself. It had exploited the riches of nature too far. Quietly and complacently, it was sinking into decadence, and progress had come to mean the progress of the Machine.” One day, of course, <i>the machine stops</i>.<br /><br />A visionary short story. I suspect Houellebecq had precisely this book in mind when imagining the future in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/996096.La_Possibilit__d_une__le" title="La Possibilité d'une île by Michel Houellebecq" rel="nofollow noopener">La Possibilité d'une île</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16446620383079529798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_989284122" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/989284122" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/989284122?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer989284122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15091067137240202364">Rating: 3.5* of five<br/><br/><b>2020 UPDATE</b> I think a lot of ear-readers will enjoy <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nJk9gk9Ow4I' rel='nofollow noopener'>this audio drama</a> of <i>The Machine Stops</i>.<br/><br/><b>COVID-19 UPDATE</b> I re-read this tale in light of the frustrations of quarantine. I have to say that, while I can't oooch a scoche higher than three-and-a-half stars, I *can* round up instead of down because the experience of reading it is much more surprising.<br/><br/><b>The Publisher Says</b>: <i>The Machine Stops</i> is a science fiction short story (12,300 words) by E. M. Forster. After initial publication i</span>
  <span id="freeText15091067137240202364" style="display:none">Rating: 3.5* of five<br><br><b>2020 UPDATE</b> I think a lot of ear-readers will enjoy <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nJk9gk9Ow4I" rel="nofollow noopener">this audio drama</a> of <i>The Machine Stops</i>.<br><br><b>COVID-19 UPDATE</b> I re-read this tale in light of the frustrations of quarantine. I have to say that, while I can't oooch a scoche higher than three-and-a-half stars, I *can* round up instead of down because the experience of reading it is much more surprising.<br><br><b>The Publisher Says</b>: <i>The Machine Stops</i> is a science fiction short story (12,300 words) by E. M. Forster. After initial publication in <i>The Oxford and Cambridge Review</i> (November 1909), the story was republished in Forster's <i>The Eternal Moment and Other Stories</i> in 1928. After being voted one of the best novellas up to 1965, it was included that same year in the populist anthology <i>Modern Short Stories</i>. In 1973 it was also included in <i>The Science Fiction Hall of Fame, Volume Two</i>. The book is particularly notable for predicting new technologies such as instant messaging and the internet.<br><br><b>My Review</b>: As amazing as reading about the Internet, streaming video, and instant messaging must have been in 1909, it's more amazing in 2014 to think that, 105 years ago, the ubiquity of such central facets of out lives was placed in a far distant future.<br><br>I mean really, what's 105 years in the sweep of history? It's even less impressive measured in geologic time. Electric lighting was still in its infancy then, though, and the automobile was a plaything for the very rich. Much like computers in 1989 and the Internet in 1999.<br><br>Well then. Sobering perspective on the speed of change in the modern world, eh what?<br><br>What mars this read for me is the vast amount of SFnal reading I've done in my life. Unlike the readers of 1909, I've imbibed the waters of the Styx and forgotten more than they ever knew about things predictive. And the trope of "civilization is making meat-sacks of us all, woe woe" has moved from startling insight and clarion warning to the dreary moaning of the Longface Puritans League that says not to eat anything that has any taste, do anything that is remotely fun, and NEVER EVER EVER have sex. Be miserable, it builds character, as Calvin's father would say! <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1584814749i/29142835._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Live longer, as Doctor Oz would say!<br><br>What ever for?<br><br>Anyway, <a href="https://manybooks.net/titles/forstereother07machine_stops.html?__cf_chl_jschl_tk__=12dbef640be960bee716fce24e1ee96014e0b3c6-1584814844-0-AZSPPUQFM20GxK1Da0HAXVZS2-E_oSZ7BXwQ6rP7b23DmcVcyBkhHabIiTQG6e665BkiKKjBU_42kQZfGZxi5jfgG7TJfZCzWjWKI01o3LzewqghLpKwtdo3PD7qA7fky4VpW-CShnjzB59S4UTB4YxDzRrL6n6W5l_j4try1K1qsX_KfQ7zUcajx70m-k_q4i59SGp5F4v-i4QCAVu17rIQpqHPTasi8MWt2cX0Rb3gqaXdo-kQ12suViyDv1JZgltFJruoq-9vDfQ5Z53oafZ7_2ICrabyLq2hLhEJF4wcxP3kwRGzMs3TfP07N5XvPuwMYAwOoDh9KeumN6y8TFU" rel="nofollow noopener">the story's free here</a> and it'll take about a half-hour to read. I come down on the side of that being a worthwhile investment. Barely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15091067137240202364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_87024610" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87024610" />
    <a title="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio"><img alt="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363779897p2/1447864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87024610?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshua Nomen-Mutatio" href="/user/show/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio">Joshua Nomen-Mutatio</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=dystopic">dystopic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1447864-joshua-nomen-mutatio?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87024610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16924790889254758769">Leonardo da Vinci famously anticipated the advent of helicopters, scuba gear, and automobiles, and had well-laid plans for primitive versions of these things.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380331325i/692606._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>(Da Vinci also used <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirror_writing' rel='nofollow noopener'>mirror writing</a> in his notebooks.)<br/><br/>The revolutionary astronomer, Johannes Kepler, similarly wrote of the invention of rocket ships traveling outside of the Earth and this was in the 1620's. This can be found in his novella <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5984974.Somnium_The_Dream_or_Posthumous_Work_on_Lunar_Astronomy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dream</a>, which is a work that is widely regarded by literary scholars and historians as the first e</span>
  <span id="freeText16924790889254758769" style="display:none">Leonardo da Vinci famously anticipated the advent of helicopters, scuba gear, and automobiles, and had well-laid plans for primitive versions of these things.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380331325i/692606._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>(Da Vinci also used <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirror_writing" rel="nofollow noopener">mirror writing</a> in his notebooks.)<br><br>The revolutionary astronomer, Johannes Kepler, similarly wrote of the invention of rocket ships traveling outside of the Earth and this was in the 1620's. This can be found in his novella <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5984974.Somnium_The_Dream_or_Posthumous_Work_on_Lunar_Astronomy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dream</a>, which is a work that is widely regarded by literary scholars and historians as the first example of writing that fits into the science fiction genre.<br><br>Following in this tradition of ingenuity and jaw-dropping foresight, E.M. Forster's <i>The Machine Stops</i> ranks along side as an amazingly prophetic story. Written in 1909 Forster anticipates the television, video conferencing and the internet and its attendant Age. <br><br>On top of this impressiveness it's also a pretty decent apocalyptic adventure story. Were this written today I'd consider its attitudes towards machinery to be naïvely Luddite and a wee bit much in the fear-mongering department, but given its historical contingencies I'm capable of seeing it in a more admirable light.<br><br>It's ultimately a nightmarish vision of a world more or less drained of human warmth and meaningfulness by the totalitarian clank and clatter of steel beams and inexorably greased engines. In a slightly reaching way it's like the inverse of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81048719" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Road</i>
</a>, if that makes any sense. The world is turned upside down by functioning machines rather than their collapse. In both scenarios humans become rather powerless. Ultimately, as the title suggests, the machine does stop and since it had gradually become so highly automated and systematically complex no one even knows how to repair it. And thus the fascistic steel world of instant gratification and decadence erodes into <i>The Road</i>ish terrority.<br><br>(It's a quick and entertaining read. Give it a shot. It can be read online <a href="http://www.plexus.org/forster/index.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16924790889254758769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2398774219?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

  <span id="freeText9094646848017253694" style="display:none">I was straightforwardly gobsmacked when I first read this story last year. Wow. Here is our world, described one hundred years before it happens. These are just a few samples that particularly appealed to me. I don’t want to give away the story and there are lots of other interesting ideas about the future, including, indeed, the idea of the idea that I will leave you to discover for yourselves,<br /><br /><blockquote><br />"Who is it?" she called. Her voice was irritable, for she had been interrupted often since the music began. She knew several thousand people, in certain directions human intercourse had advanced enormously. But when she listened into the receiver, her white face wrinkled into smiles, and she said: "Very well. Let us talk, I will isolate myself. I do not expect anything important will happen for the next five minutes-for I can give you fully five minutes, Kuno. <br /></blockquote><br /><br />Modern life indeed.<br /><br />On the subject of us accepting what is inferior but convenient, interpolating the machine in our relationships with each other.<br /><br /><blockquote><br />In this world all people live in isolation in their rooms with technology supplying everything. Kuno is her son and wishes to see her. When she exclaims that he is seeing her, he replies:<br /><br />“I see something like you in this plate, but I do not see you. I hear something like you through this telephone, but I do not hear you. That is why I want you to come." <br /></blockquote><br /><br />And <br /><br /><blockquote><br />The clumsy system of public gatherings had been long since abandoned; neither Vashti nor her audience stirred from their rooms. Seated in her armchair she spoke, while they in their armchairs heard her, fairly well, and saw her, fairly well.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />On globalisation:<br /><br /><blockquote><br />Few travelled in these days, for, thanks to the advance of science, the earth was exactly alike all over. Rapid intercourse, from which the previous civilization had hoped so much, had ended by defeating itself. What was the good of going to Peking when it was just like Shrewsbury? Why return to Shrewsbury when it would all be like Peking? Men seldom moved their bodies; all unrest was concentrated in the soul.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />On modern selection of foetuses, which shall live and which shall die, a process during which we believe ourselves to be morally correct:<br /><br /><blockquote><br />By these days it was a demerit to be muscular. Each infant was examined at birth, and all who promised undue strength were destroyed. Humanitarians may protest, but it would have been no true kindness to let an athlete live; he would never have been happy in that state of life to which the Machine had called him; he would have yearned for trees to climb, rivers to bathe in, meadows and hills against which he might measure his body. Man must be adapted to his surroundings, must he not?<br /></blockquote><br /><br />And, close to my heart, on the nature of the revision of history according to the contemporary mores of the revisionist:<br /><br />rest here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://alittleteaalittlechat.wordpress.com/2019/11/09/the-machine-stops-by-em-forster/">https://alittleteaalittlechat.wordpre...</a></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141195988" href="/book/show/9243302-the-machine-stops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76202320-henk?shelf=owned">owned</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3382304037" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3577682668784915814"><b>The Machine Stops is quite well done and prescient, The Celestial Omnibus was less my thing</b><br/><i>Thing went from bad to worse unchallenged.</i><br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4711854.The_Machine_Stops' title='The Machine Stops by E.M. Forster' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Machine Stops</a> in 1928 brutally foreshadowed a world filled with a kind of internet, VR, webinars and always being reachable and busy, with all associated risk of humanity fully relying on technology for its survival. The fall of civilization starts with no longer relying on firsthand experiences (which also destroys the empire in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29579.Foundation__Foundation___1_' title='Foundation (Foundation, #1) by Isaac Asimov' rel='nofollow noopener'>Foundation</a> of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16667.Isaac_Asimov' title='Isaac Asimov' rel='nofollow noopener'>Isaac Asimov</a> that</span>
  <span id="freeText3577682668784915814" style="display:none"><b>The Machine Stops is quite well done and prescient, The Celestial Omnibus was less my thing</b><br /><i>Thing went from bad to worse unchallenged.</i><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4711854.The_Machine_Stops" title="The Machine Stops by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">The Machine Stops</a> in 1928 brutally foreshadowed a world filled with a kind of internet, VR, webinars and always being reachable and busy, with all associated risk of humanity fully relying on technology for its survival. The fall of civilization starts with no longer relying on firsthand experiences (which also destroys the empire in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29579.Foundation__Foundation___1_" title="Foundation (Foundation, #1) by Isaac Asimov" rel="nofollow noopener">Foundation</a> of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16667.Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov" rel="nofollow noopener">Isaac Asimov</a> that I am reading at the moment).<br /><br />It makes you think what would happen if in our interconnected, stay at home Covid-19 world, the internet would just simply stop working.<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/86404.E_M__Forster" title="E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">E.M. Forster</a> has no qualms on what will happen in such a case, The Machine Stops is a gloomy but all to believable tale of alienation and overreliance on things we don't understand anymore (<i>Loud were the complains, impotent the remedies</i>).<br /><br />Celestial Omnibus from 1911 revolves around the power of poetry, youth and imagination versus formal knowledge of the arts. I found it a bit twee and this led to the stories jointly being a three star read for me. But definitely pick up The Machine Stops for the literary equivalent of a Dark Mirror episode; the title story is very well done!</span>

<div id="review_2454318660" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2454318660" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16077597-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582900704p2/16077597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2454318660?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/16077597-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/36303986-the-machine-stops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=media-ebooks">media-ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=hard-rain">hard-rain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2454318660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8493513370923878867">An amazingly accurate prediction of today’s alienation from life and its artificiality through technology and the internet. <br/><br/>In E.M. Forster’s future society people withdraw from real life, spending their days and nights in a single subterranean room (which looks more like a cell or a honeycomb) surrounded by buttons to press and levers to pull and also some kind of communication apparatus to contact other people. Leaving one’s room is possible and not forbidden, but hardly anyone does it. Travel</span>
  <span id="freeText8493513370923878867" style="display:none">An amazingly accurate prediction of today’s alienation from life and its artificiality through technology and the internet. <br><br>In E.M. Forster’s future society people withdraw from real life, spending their days and nights in a single subterranean room (which looks more like a cell or a honeycomb) surrounded by buttons to press and levers to pull and also some kind of communication apparatus to contact other people. Leaving one’s room is possible and not forbidden, but hardly anyone does it. Travelling in trains under ground or in “Air-Ships” high above the clouds and mountains are the only way you can go from place to place. The surface of the Earth has become uninhabitable.<br><br>Physical needs are provided for by THE MACHINE which also runs the gadgets in the rooms and controls communication channels. People are spending their waking time passing on “ideas” in the form of short lectures to other people or are in turn being lectured by them. First-hand ideas from direct observations are being frowned upon (where should those come from anyway?), but second-hand ideas or better yet tenth-hand are fine.<br><br>One day a woman, who developed an almost religious adoration for THE MACHINE, receives a call from one of her sons on the other side of the world. He requests the unthinkable from his mother: “Come, and visit me.”<br><br>The author apparently deliberately kept the description of the actual technology of THE MACHINE somewhat vague. A smart move, because his novella remains worth reading even after more than a hundred years have passed since publication. If I would accurately describe the state of the Internet today in a story, people would probably laugh me down in a 100 years, or 50 or 20 or 10 years. Who can know for sure? Exponential progress can be such a cruel thing. You have to be careful to not get under the wheels and be considered a misfit. Better keep your Facebook entries up-to-date and your messenger on your phone online all the time and ready to beep you off your thoughts. And always - always! - keep an eye on newer developments that might improve your life even a bit more. This way you also have fodder for conversation with virtual “friends”.<br><br>I realize that writing a review of a book that pretty much condemns the so called “social” media on a social media platform is somewhat absurd. I should rather go outside, meet a stranger in a pub, give them the book and say “Here, read this and get your life back. It’s the only one you have and there won’t be WhatsApp in the afterlife.” But, alas, I bought the electronic version of the book (which is not transferable) from a machine called <em>Amazon</em> and the pub has been replaced with an internet café.<br>_________________________________<br><em>[Update, Mar 21 2020]</em><br><br>Forget what I said about going out and meeting new people. There's a whole new ballgame. It's called #FlattenTheCurve and it simply involves spending your time in front of a machine lecturing people about staying home.<br><br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8493513370923878867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_961062826" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/961062826" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/961062826?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=fave-classics">fave-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer961062826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9374676938489703056"><blockquote>
  <i>“You talk as if a god had made the Machine," cried the other. "I believe that you pray to it when you are unhappy. Men made it, do not forget that. Great men, but men. The Machine is much, but not everything.” </i>
</blockquote>E.M. Forster could have been talking about Steve Jobs and iPhones!<br/><br/>I don't know how widely read <i>The Machine Stops</i> is but I think it ought to be required reading for all sci-fi aficionados. I don't know this for a fact but I suspect it is very influential; I can see echoes of it in sci-f</span>
  <span id="freeText9374676938489703056" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“You talk as if a god had made the Machine," cried the other. "I believe that you pray to it when you are unhappy. Men made it, do not forget that. Great men, but men. The Machine is much, but not everything.” </i>
</blockquote>E.M. Forster could have been talking about Steve Jobs and iPhones!<br><br>I don't know how widely read <i>The Machine Stops</i> is but I think it ought to be required reading for all sci-fi aficionados. I don't know this for a fact but I suspect it is very influential; I can see echoes of it in sci-fi classics such as Harlan Ellison’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/415459.I_Have_No_Mouth_and_I_Must_Scream" title="I Have No Mouth and I Must Scream by Harlan Ellison" rel="nofollow noopener">I Have No Mouth and I Must Scream</a>, Asimov’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41811.The_Caves_of_Steel__Robot__1_" title="The Caves of Steel (Robot #1) by Isaac Asimov" rel="nofollow noopener">The Caves of Steel</a>, the classic short story “Nightfall” and even in something as recent as Hugh Howey’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/946397851?utm_medium=email&amp;utm_source=rating" rel="nofollow noopener">Wool</a>. Perhaps even Iain Banks’ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/49118-culture" rel="nofollow noopener">Culture series</a> to some extent.<br><br>This is a proto-dystopian sci-fi story first published in 1909. Here we have the human race all living underground to avoid exposure to a polluted poisonous Earth atmosphere (though things may not be as they seem). Mankind is totally ruled and nurtured by a global AI Overlord simply called The Machine. Everybody’s material needs and creature comforts can be conjured as needed (presumably backsides are also wiped). <br><br>So what happens when the entire human race is totally reliant on a single supercomputer* to the extent that their muscles have atrophied and decadence has set it? I'm not going to tell you obviously but this is a brilliant prescient story that I hope you will read. <br><br>Pity <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/86404.E_M__Forster" title="E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">E.M. Forster</a> only wrote this one sci-fi story, he really had a knack for it. He is of course best known for classics like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3087.A_Room_with_a_View" title="A Room with a View by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">A Room with a View</a> (which I have read and reviewed) and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45195.A_Passage_to_India" title="A Passage to India by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">A Passage to India</a> (which I read years ago, and is very good) etc. <i>The Machine Stops</i> is so good that it makes me want to read more of his books regardless of genre. <br><br>This story is in the public domain and you can find a free e-book edition at <a href="http://manybooks.net/titles/forstereother07machine_stops.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Manybooks.net</a> and a free audiobook at <a href="https://librivox.org/the-machine-stops-version-3-by-e-m-forster/" rel="nofollow noopener">Librivox</a>. Now all you need is an hour or so to soak it all up!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1502679486i/23583644._SX540_.jpg" alt="fancy line" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>* The word "computer" does not appear anywhere in the text of course, 1909 y'know, that makes his prescience even more remarkable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9374676938489703056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
