<div id="review_1030843297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1030843297" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1030843297?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6293410-the-machine-stops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-e-h">read-authors-e-h</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=sf-speculative">sf-speculative</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1030843297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer551931524642594518">Where would you be without the Internet? Can you imagine your life? Can you even remember a time before personal computers?<br/><br/><i>"The machine stops."</i><br/><br/>Disaster! What a thought! Did you breathe a sigh of relief when the Internet seemed to carry on as normal after the millennium date? That computer technology had not broken down because of bad programming after all? Surely there had been just that smidgen of a possibility...<br/><br/><b>The Machine Stops</b> is a remarkably prescient science fiction short novella by E. M.</span>
  <span id="freeText551931524642594518" style="display:none">Where would you be without the Internet? Can you imagine your life? Can you even remember a time before personal computers?<br /><br /><i>"The machine stops."</i><br /><br />Disaster! What a thought! Did you breathe a sigh of relief when the Internet seemed to carry on as normal after the millennium date? That computer technology had not broken down because of bad programming after all? Surely there had been just that smidgen of a possibility...<br /><br /><b>The Machine Stops</b> is a remarkably prescient science fiction short novella by E. M. Forster. In the world he describes, humans live underground, as the surface of the Earth is thought to be unsuitable or dangerous. They rarely venture out, and have lost the ability to live their daily lives, or even to communicate except by virtue of an everpresent powerful and global "Machine". <br /><br /><i>"The clumsy system of public gatherings had been long since abandoned; neither Vashti nor her audience stirred from their rooms."</i><br /><br />Each person lives in isolation in an individual <i>"cell"</i>, and although we learn that the isolated cells are actually situated adjacent to other people's cells, all communication is nevertheless through the machine. There are buttons and switches everywhere. Everything is available at the touch of a button - food, music, clothing - whatever you like. It is a highly-mechanised world.<br /><br />Ideas are much valued, and people spend much of their time chatting and lecturing on <i>"ideas"</i>. They communicate by looking into a <i>"round, blue plate"</i>, or in our terms, a screen or a monitor. Knowledge has become skewed by the people's reverence of the Machine. First-hand experience is not to be trusted. Everything must be considered according to the relevance of their lives ruled by the Machine. <br /><br />The novella is divided into three sections and we learn that over time <i>"The Book of the Machine"</i> begins to be ever more revered. The story refers to a <i>"re-establishment"</i> of religion, with the development of arcane ceremonies and ritualistic chanting, and thus the Machine itself becomes god-like and to be worshipped. <br /><br /><i>"They described the strange feeling of peace when they handled the Book of the Machine."</i><br /><br />Gradually any life support apparatus needed to visit the outer world is dismantled and eventually abolished. People begin to forget that humans created the Machine. Anyone who questions the supreme power and authority of the Machine is viewed as <i>"unmechanical"</i> and threatened with <i>"Homelessness"</i>, which would be expulsion from the underground environment, therefore, presumably death. The title has already informed the reader of what apocalyptic event will happen. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The <i>"Mending Apparatus"</i> will also begin to fail. The Machine after all is not omnipotent. It is a mere machine. Once the knowledge of how to repair the Machine has been lost, it will collapse, taking the so-called "civilisation" with it. Millions of humans will die and it will be the few surface-dwellers who still exist who rebuild the human race. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />At what point did this scenario begin to seem unfamiliar? About halfway through? Certainly aspects of this have already come to pass. We may still get out and about, but it is quite possible that the next person you see will be engrossed by their Machine, whether they are across the room from you, across the road, across the park, across the railway carriage. They are absorbed in their own world; their own <i>"cell"</i>, aren't they?<br /><br />So it is quite remarkable that E. M. Forster's dystopian vision was published in <i>"The Oxford and Cambridge Review"</i> in 1909. He had a prophetic vision of how a Machine could lead to disaster, over a century ago. It became part of a collection of short stories which took its name from another story, entitled, <i>"The Eternal Moment"</i>, subsequently published in 1928. This was well before the Internet, personal computers, instant messaging, emails, videoconferencing and many of the other functions he describes with such accuracy, which have come to pass. As well as gramophones, which Forster will have known about, he predicted <i>"cinematophoes"</i> - machines to project visual images - of which we now have a proliferation, but called by other names. We even have "cells" to live in, which may all look alike, in a tower block for instance. Our city centres are look the same. We are dependent on technology. We have lost the quality of patience and calm; our devices have to be repaired or replaced instantly. Even the faceless <i>"Committees"</i> towards the end of the story, making empty promises of assistance, seem sadly familiar. <br /><br />The first time I came across this story was as part of the <i>"Out of the Unknown"</i> Science Fiction series on British television, in 1966. It captured my imagination even then. At that point in time the story resonated, some aspects seemed to be eerily foreshadowing, yet the whole still seemed impossibly futuristic. Yet even so, it was over 50 years since it had first been published. How much more similar the world is now, to Forster's vision. How much more familiar the events.<br /><br />E. M. Forster's story concentrates on two individuals. One is Vashti, an "Everyman" character typical of her age and society. The other is her son, the rebel, Kuno, who lives on the opposite side of the world. Such a physical distance is the norm for this society based on maintaining individuals, not families.<br /><br /><i>"I want to see you not through the machine," said Kuno. "I want to speak to you not through the wearisome Machine." "Oh Hush!", said his mother, vaguely shocked. "You mustn't say anything against the machine."</i><br /><br />Kuno has a glimmering of a world outside, and tries to alert others to what might happen. He wants to see the Earth's surface. He wants to live outside the Machine. He wants to see if there are others. Will he? The story concentrates on Vashti, who is blinkered by her conditioning. She regards Kuno's ideas as <i>"dangerous madness"</i>. Although the Machine was originally constructed to save the human race, lack of human interaction has led to a dependency on it. The story describes what we might call her "brainwashing", her reactions to the catastrophic events which follow, and her brief connection with Kuno.<br /> <br />E. M. Forster puts our humanity squarely back into focus at the end of the story. In the preface to a later collection, he wrote that,<br /><br /><i>"The Machine Stops" is a reaction to one of the earlier heavens of H. G. Wells."</i> <br /><br />Clearly this implies that Forster was concerned about human dependence on technology in the future. In the final analysis, we learn that it is our humanity itself which is valuable, and that this should be our measure of things. An over-reliance on technology leads to humans becomes subservient to Machines. Acceptance of the domination of Machines would lead to corruption, a debasement of values, and eventually to a society that has no chance of survival. It is a chilling and sobering tale, and should probably be read by anyone who ever uses the Internet, which in the end may possibly be us all.<br /><br /><i>"The machine is stopping, I know it, I know the signs!"</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="551931524642594518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2535319056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2535319056" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2535319056?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2018-shelf">2018-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2535319056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11385279027907479922">I was honestly surprised as hell to learn from my GR buddies that this old classic fantastic traditional fiction author wrote an SF novella. EM FORSTER???<br/><br/>Well, yeah! Contemporary of Wells, this particular novel seems to be a rather delightful stick to poke at the culture that spawned it. <br/><br/>Fear of the Machine, perhaps only Bureaucracy, but probably a lot more as in the kind of AI the world has become, I was more impressed with the snide comments it had about eschewing primary sources over a consta</span>
  <span id="freeText11385279027907479922" style="display:none">I was honestly surprised as hell to learn from my GR buddies that this old classic fantastic traditional fiction author wrote an SF novella. EM FORSTER???<br /><br />Well, yeah! Contemporary of Wells, this particular novel seems to be a rather delightful stick to poke at the culture that spawned it. <br /><br />Fear of the Machine, perhaps only Bureaucracy, but probably a lot more as in the kind of AI the world has become, I was more impressed with the snide comments it had about eschewing primary sources over a constant rehash of old ideas. :) <br /><br />Now, of course, this came out in 1909 so I have to wonder if it inspired so many similar tales to come after. I'm reminded of Morlocks, but better than that... there's a little gem of a film that came out 20 years later called Metropolis that seems to be the best fit. :)<br /><br />Never mind Tolkien and his condemnation of machinist thinking in The Ring, or even the Truman Show to break out of his virtual shell to see the world by first principles and first experience. :)<br /><br />Honorable mention in the novella goes to the ideas of the videophone and the internet and AI's. :) Yes. 1909. :) And machine overlords, of course. :) Pretty awesome. But that's just for the ideas.<br /><br />The writing, alas, is not quite as evocative as his traditional fiction. Oddly enough. Still, quite good. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11385279027907479922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2219454365" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2219454365" />
    <a title="mark monday" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday"><img alt="mark monday" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572672245p2/131922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2219454365?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mark monday" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mark monday" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday">mark monday</a>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2219454365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5119245578529002817">Future is now; future is then. Old Man Forster decries the cold meaningless of life in the age of the world wide web and automation and being repulsed by another human's touch. He shakes a well-manicured fist at the new millennium, at 2017, except he shook that fist over a century ago, while no doubt wearing an elegant three-piece suit, with ascot, as he held court in his finely wallpapered drawing room. Could Grampa see into now? It sure seems like it. I really get Gramps and his carefully word</span>
  <span id="freeText5119245578529002817" style="display:none">Future is now; future is then. Old Man Forster decries the cold meaningless of life in the age of the world wide web and automation and being repulsed by another human's touch. He shakes a well-manicured fist at the new millennium, at 2017, except he shook that fist over a century ago, while no doubt wearing an elegant three-piece suit, with ascot, as he held court in his finely wallpapered drawing room. Could Grampa see into now? It sure seems like it. I really get Gramps and his carefully worded anger. He wrote an amazingly prescient short story; a sour, bitter little triumph.<br><br>Read this short story for free! Right here:<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://archive.ncsa.illinois.edu/prajlich/forster.html">http://archive.ncsa.illinois.edu/praj...</a><br><br><a href="http://s181.photobucket.com/user/markmonday/media/tumblr_mrf23ojK9e1qzsvlyo1_500_zpsotjtxhi7.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1513762310i/24719133._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo tumblr_mrf23ojK9e1qzsvlyo1_500_zpsotjtxhi7.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>The machine stops, and so a happy ending is reached. A happy ending which means the destruction of all civilization so that we can start anew. That made me smile.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5119245578529002817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2219454365">
  <td colspan="2">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1720097362?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dannii Elle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dannii Elle" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle">Dannii Elle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/26810960-the-machine-stops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44487975-dannii-elle?shelf=satirical-susceptiveness">satirical-susceptiveness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44487975-dannii-elle?shelf=science-fiction-sagacity">science-fiction-sagacity</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1720097362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer889206041796451779">It is absolutely astonishing to me that something written over 100 years ago can have such relevance in today's society. Whilst short, this science fiction masterpiece manages to create a future society that is not dissimilar to our own, in many respects.<br/><br/>This satirical look at a world run by machinery feels like it could quite possibility represent our own bleak future, as we already have apps and technology to make every aspect of our lives easier. This only progresses our current state by maki</span>
  <span id="freeText889206041796451779" style="display:none">It is absolutely astonishing to me that something written over 100 years ago can have such relevance in today's society. Whilst short, this science fiction masterpiece manages to create a future society that is not dissimilar to our own, in many respects.<br /><br />This satirical look at a world run by machinery feels like it could quite possibility represent our own bleak future, as we already have apps and technology to make every aspect of our lives easier. This only progresses our current state by making <i>every</i> physical and mental movement predicted and provided for by the governing body of 'the machine'. Even human interaction has been spared and the citizens of the world live isolated and sedentary lives, where their only relationships happen over video-based technology and any personal, scientific and theoretic progression has stalled.<br /><br />That the idea was even conceived for this, so long ago, is astounding but that the idea has been fleshed out so completely, in such a limited number of pages, and so beautifully, by the evocative writing style, makes this a very deserving science fiction classic!</span>
  <a data-text-id="889206041796451779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2404724880" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2404724880" />
    <a title="Mackey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28470777-mackey"><img alt="Mackey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562878870p2/28470777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2404724880?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mackey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mackey" href="/user/show/28470777-mackey">Mackey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28470777-mackey?shelf=books-read-2018">books-read-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2404724880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16446610483888661331"><b>“Let your ideas be second-hand, and if possible tenth-hand, for then they will be far removed from that disturbing element – direct observation. Do not learn anything about this subject of mine – the French Revolution. Learn instead what I think that Enicharmon thought Urizen thought Gutch thought Ho-Yung thought Chi-Bo-Sing thought Lafcadio Hearn thought Carlyle thought Mirabeau said about the French Revolution.”</b><br/>― E.M. Forster, The Machine Stops<br/><br/>The first thing you will notice when reading E.M F</span>
  <span id="freeText16446610483888661331" style="display:none"><b>“Let your ideas be second-hand, and if possible tenth-hand, for then they will be far removed from that disturbing element – direct observation. Do not learn anything about this subject of mine – the French Revolution. Learn instead what I think that Enicharmon thought Urizen thought Gutch thought Ho-Yung thought Chi-Bo-Sing thought Lafcadio Hearn thought Carlyle thought Mirabeau said about the French Revolution.”</b><br />― E.M. Forster, The Machine Stops<br /><br />The first thing you will notice when reading E.M Forster’s “The Machine Stops,” is how eerily real it seems for a Science Fiction novella. You may even ask yourself “what is all of the hype about machines when they are all around us?” That is because you are reading this book today, 2018, but it was written over 100 years ago! While many hail Wells and Bradbury for their insightful vision into the future, here is Forster describing with inexplicable accuracy a cell phone, the internet, holographic imagery, 3D printers and, most frightening of all, the destruction of the earth due to climate change. Remember, this is prior to the ill-effects of the Industrial Revolution. Ahhh, if only people had read and learned.<br /><br />Of course, as with anything that man has created, man must also maintain and that includes The Machine. But man, having to do nothing, where even lifting a real book is too strenuous for the atrophied arms, also becomes a bit atrophied and the machine glitches. What will happen to this perfect world then?<br /><br />While many who read this book are astonished at the degree of technology that Forster created, I was mesmerized by the clarity in which he “predicted” human behavior. People no longer traveled for pleasure – there was no point when pictures on their screens were better, clearer than real life. They no longer actually read books but read what others wrote about the books – aaaahhh, not too different from here, wouldn’t you agree? In the end, they no longer relied on the “truth,” but rather another person’s version of the truth – even yet, the more interpretations of an event, the better. After all, an eyewitness to an event might actually be too easily influenced by the event itself. Perhaps the politicians of today actually did the novella and that is where they got the idea of “fake news,” for what is Facebook and other social media outlets if not the re-telling of the story through multiple sources?<br /><br />Sadly, the story – like real life – does not end well. I cannot help but wonder if anyone who reads the book today sees the reality of their own fate on these pages. What happens to mankind when The Machine Stops?<br /><br />This is a short novella; it took me a few hours to read at the most. I encourage you to download it – it is available for free from multiple sources. It truly is remarkable.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16446610483888661331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87123553" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87123553?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87123553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4476196057350736538">After I'd read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113257800' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>A Rebours</i>
</a> last week, it occurred to me to wonder whether this well-known novella was yet another piece that linked to it. The main theme in Huysmans's book is the superiority of the artificial: the engagingly mad des Esseintes tries to construct an entirely artificial world to live in, and constantly explains how much better it is than the real one. For example, steam locomotives are much sexier than women. <br/><br/>Huysmans's presentation is completely deadpan, so it's difficult to kn</span>
  <span id="freeText4476196057350736538" style="display:none">After I'd read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113257800" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>A Rebours</i>
</a> last week, it occurred to me to wonder whether this well-known novella was yet another piece that linked to it. The main theme in Huysmans's book is the superiority of the artificial: the engagingly mad des Esseintes tries to construct an entirely artificial world to live in, and constantly explains how much better it is than the real one. For example, steam locomotives are much sexier than women. <br /><br />Huysmans's presentation is completely deadpan, so it's difficult to know how ironic he means to be; anyway, it's not impossible to read the book straight, and maybe Forster felt that an unambiguous response was necessary. Here, the world has reached the end of the road Huysmans envisages. Everything is completely artificial and people love it like that. But, in this version, it is made abundantly clear that excessive artifice is not good.<br /><br />I'm trying to think which one I prefer! It's difficult. They're both very memorable in their rather different ways. Huysmans's style is more compelling, but you get a rather less complicated form of pleasure from Forster. Really, if you've only read one of them and liked it, you're almost obliged to read the other.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4476196057350736538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1518827829" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11727158711372606203">First published in 1909, this science-fiction novella by Forster is a futuristic, dystopian view of society controlled by a machine. Everyone lives underground, the Earths surface no longer habitable, and everything is interconnected by something that sounds like our computers and Internet of today. Forster is trying to tell us not to become to dependent on technology. I imagine he would be shocked at the "progress" we've made in 100 years.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2282711420">

<div id="review_1539434666" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1539434666" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1539434666?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=short-stories-novellas">short-stories-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=breakfast-club">breakfast-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1539434666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6307221673312099134">E. M. Forster's dystopian tale of human beings living beneath the surface of the earth, dependent on a large machine to serve all their needs. There is little or no contact between people other than through the phone (like Skype) where they see and speak with one another. Lives are inactive and purposeless. <br/><br/>What fascinated me the most about this story was the fact that it was written in 1909 and yet it foreshadows an overly dependent world of technology, that is sometimes all too real today. The</span>
  <span id="freeText6307221673312099134" style="display:none">E. M. Forster's dystopian tale of human beings living beneath the surface of the earth, dependent on a large machine to serve all their needs. There is little or no contact between people other than through the phone (like Skype) where they see and speak with one another. Lives are inactive and purposeless. <br /><br />What fascinated me the most about this story was the fact that it was written in 1909 and yet it foreshadows an overly dependent world of technology, that is sometimes all too real today. The ideas here might not seem earth-shattering, in that many of them have been explored in other places, but this is surely among one of the first presentations and deserves some credit and audience.<br /><br />I find these kinds of stories more frightening than supernatural ones. This particular scenario might be far-fetched, but something of its kind seems to me to be all too achievable by mankind.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100063363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15028434492438106602">4.0 to 4.5 stars. A classic, haunting short story (more like a novella) written in 1909. The story concerns a world in which humanity (long ago) lost the ability to live on the surface of the Earth (through War, disease, etc.). Now each person lives in almost complete "isolation" below ground in a "cell" where all their needs are taken care of by "the Machine." The Machine is an advanced computer created by humans in the distant past to assist mankind and on which mankind continued to rely on mo</span>
  <span id="freeText15028434492438106602" style="display:none">4.0 to 4.5 stars. A classic, haunting short story (more like a novella) written in 1909. The story concerns a world in which humanity (long ago) lost the ability to live on the surface of the Earth (through War, disease, etc.). Now each person lives in almost complete "isolation" below ground in a "cell" where all their needs are taken care of by "the Machine." The Machine is an advanced computer created by humans in the distant past to assist mankind and on which mankind continued to rely on more and more until they became totaly dependant upon it. At the time of the story, people do not like to travel, all communicaiton is conducted via (can you believe it) instant messaging and video conferencing (remember this was written in 1909) and people are no longer "intellectually" curious. <br /><br />The plot involves a member of the society who begins to distrust the way society is living, longs to visit the surface of the world and be free from the "assistance" of the Machine. I won't give any more away, but this is a powerful story (again written over 100 years ago) about the possible dangers of becoming over dependent on technology. <br /><br />This story was included in the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/219456.The_Science_Fiction_Hall_of_Fame_Volume_2B" title="The Science Fiction Hall of Fame Volume 2B by Ben Bova" rel="nofollow noopener">The Science Fiction Hall of Fame: Volume 2B</a>. HIGHLY RECOMMENDED.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15028434492438106602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1461258200" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1461258200" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1461258200?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781519222145" href="/book/show/27978051-the-machine-stops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fantasy-horror">fantasy-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=short-fiction">short-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1461258200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer678105428863995957">Imagine a world -- one that could have been created by H.G. Wells -- in which people live under the earth in comfortable caves in which everything, from food to air to entertainment to sleep, is controlled by a vast machine:<blockquote>Imagine, if you can, a small room, hexagonal in shape, like the cell of a bee. It is lighted neither by window nor by lamp, yet it is filled with a soft radiance. There are no apertures for ventilation, yet the air is fresh. There are no musical instruments, and yet, at the m</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText678105428863995957" style="display:none">Imagine a world -- one that could have been created by H.G. Wells -- in which people live under the earth in comfortable caves in which everything, from food to air to entertainment to sleep, is controlled by a vast machine:<blockquote>Imagine, if you can, a small room, hexagonal in shape, like the cell of a bee. It is lighted neither by window nor by lamp, yet it is filled with a soft radiance. There are no apertures for ventilation, yet the air is fresh. There are no musical instruments, and yet, at the moment that my meditation opens, this room is throbbing with melodious sounds. An armchair is in the centre, by its side a reading-desk-that is all the furniture. And in the armchair there sits a swaddled lump of flesh-a woman, about five feet high, with a face as white as a fungus. It is to her that the little room belongs.</blockquote>That is our introduction to one Vashti (no last name), who is contacted by her son Kuno who has -- Gasp! -- been to the surface of the planet and seen the sun and ferns and stars, before being yanked bodily by the machine back underground.<br /><br />But think of Newton's Second Law of Thermodynamics: All machines eventually run down. And this is what happens to The Machine. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/86404.E_M__Forster" title="E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">E.M. Forster</a> has written probably one of the best and most prophetic of short stories in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4711854.The_Machine_Stops_" title="The Machine Stops  by E.M. Forster" rel="nofollow noopener">The Machine Stops</a>. I had actually never heard of it until I had a conversation with a close friend who recommended it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="678105428863995957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1687671984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3014097792821875936">Classic proto-SF story, first published in 1909 and reprinted many times. Widely recognized for predicting technologies such as instant messaging and the Internet. And still a very readable story. It was selected for the Science Fiction Hall of Fame in 1973 by the Science Fiction Writers of America. <br/><br/>If you haven't read it, or if it's been awhile:<br/>Public-domain copy available at<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Machine_Stops'>https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Ma...</a><br/>Highly recommended.</span>
  <span id="freeText3014097792821875936" style="display:none">Classic proto-SF story, first published in 1909 and reprinted many times. Widely recognized for predicting technologies such as instant messaging and the Internet. And still a very readable story. It was selected for the Science Fiction Hall of Fame in 1973 by the Science Fiction Writers of America. <br /><br />If you haven't read it, or if it's been awhile:<br />Public-domain copy available at<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Machine_Stops">https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Ma...</a><br />Highly recommended.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4dfHBwRzmGdWh+49/kGw1epNVwoDUFDL36rrCOvZdAtlIN1lGD7x5UhkIUFBJO6Xaiq1OBgcU5MXltsTHy24Iw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wqXo/Ww+dmD2LmL2X0Mij2OD3lMNUCzzfj+i6Zk2AqZGUvKfcHMf4ujNrYrgJnzN4+Q8YRYcL6u2A5LybcLOjg==')})
  }

The audacious and patchwork concept underlying this story (the richer you are the less you are forced to consume; the greatest poverty is involved with the aggregation of goods) was Horace Gold’s and according to Pohl he had offered it to almost all of his regular contributors, asking for a story centered on the idea. The idea lacks all credibility, everyone (including Pohl) told him and everyone refused to write something so patently unbelievable until, according to Pohl, Horace browbeat him into an attempt and Pohl decided that it was less trouble to deliver something than continue to resist. To his utter shock, the story was received by Gold and his readership with great glee, was among the most popular GALAXY ever published (or Pohl) and one of the most anthologized. Whether this demonstrated the audacity and scope of Gold’s unreason or whether it confirmed Gold’s genius (or both) Pohl was utterly unable to decide. The sculpted consumer-obsessed society was used again by Pohl a few years later in the novelette THE MAN WHO ATE THE WORLD which was far more credible (consumption-obsession as a kind of personal In a desolate future, she is chasing the past. “Limited Edition” takes us to a time before the crash to introduce us to Grids, College, and Melody—a tight-knit, sneaker-obsessed South Bristol crew. When a new pair of limited-edition trainers drops, they won\'t let a little thing like being broke get in their way—even if they have to do some VR hacking to pull off an IRL heist.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/86404.E_M_Forster">About E.M. Forster</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/86404.E_M_Forster"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1402057803p3/86404.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/86404.E_M_Forster">E.M. Forster
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12563445933754851542"><b>Edward Morgan Forster</b>, generally published as <b>E.M. Forster</b>, was an novelist, essayist, and short story writer. He is known best for his ironic and well-plotted novels examining class difference and hypocrisy in early 20th-century British society. His humanistic impulse toward understanding and sympathy may be aptly summed up in the epigraph to his 1910 novel <i>Howards End</i>: "Only connect".<br/><br/>He had five</span>
  <span id="freeText12563445933754851542" style="display:none"><b>Edward Morgan Forster</b>, generally published as <b>E.M. Forster</b>, was an novelist, essayist, and short story writer. He is known best for his ironic and well-plotted novels examining class difference and hypocrisy in early 20th-century British society. His humanistic impulse toward understanding and sympathy may be aptly summed up in the epigraph to his 1910 novel <i>Howards End</i>: "Only connect".<br /><br />He had five novels published in his lifetime, achieving his greatest success with <i>A Passage to India</i> (1924) which takes as its subject the relationship between East and West, seen through the lens of India in the later days of the British Raj. <br /><br />Forster's views as a secular humanist are at the heart of his work, which often depicts the pursuit of personal connections in spite of the restrictions of contemporary society. He is noted for his use of symbolism as a technique in his novels, and he has been criticised for his attachment to mysticism. His other works include <i>Where Angels Fear to Tread</i> (1905), <i>The Longest Journey</i> (1907), <i>A Room with a View</i> (1908) and <i>Maurice</i> (1971), his posthumously published novel which tells of the coming of age of an explicitly gay male character.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12563445933754851542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/4776249-the-machine-stops">Quotes from The Machine Stops</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You talk as if a god had made the Machine," cried the other. "I believe that you pray to it when you are unhappy. Men made it, do not forget that. Great men, but men. The Machine is much, but not everything.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/639552-you-talk-as-if-a-god-had-made-the-machine">29 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Few travelled in these days, for, thanks to the advance of science, the earth was exactly alike all over. Rapid intercourse, from which the previous civilization had hoped so much, had ended by defeating itself. What was the good of going to Peking when it was just like Shrewsbury? Why return to Shrewsbury when it would all be like Peking? Men seldom moved their bodies; all unrest was concentrated in the soul.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/843709-few-travelled-in-these-days-for-thanks-to-the-advance">18 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
